    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="6a133fffccfe44f8b81e43ce94df7c92">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '460f654e-bc7a-4eaa-8ad3-e53e6296fa13'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <h1 class="citation__title">High Entropy Boosts the Low Temperature Na<sup>+</sup>-Storage Performance of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub></h1>

      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d16455670" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>Herein, spherical high entropy (HE) Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.95</sub>(MgCaAlCrMn)<sub>0.01</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (HE-NFPP) cathode materials have been produced by utilizing a spray-drying combined with solid-state reaction method. The transition metals (TMs) co-doping in NFPP through HE strategy can effectively mitigate the volume change and improve its electronic conductivity by reducing the bandgap between the conduction and valence bands. While, the 3D network Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion channels formed in NFPP by TM ions co-doping through HE strategy facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion, leading to an enhanced electrode reaction kinetics at low temperatures. 

            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8e886253-eaed-4f81-8e64-784878e335fc/anie202502693-gra-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8e886253-eaed-4f81-8e64-784878e335fc/anie202502693-gra-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a10f35f5-55b5-4431-89ad-df14194ca567/anie202502693-gra-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8e886253-eaed-4f81-8e64-784878e335fc/anie202502693-gra-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure><p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d16455673" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP) is considered a promising cathode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs). However, the inferior electronic conductivity and inadequate Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion kinetics of NFPP at low temperatures hinder its practical applications. In this study, high entropy Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.95</sub>(MgCaAlCrMn)<sub>0.01</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (HE-NFPP) has been synthesized via a spray-drying and high temperature sintering technique. The incorporation of transition metal (TMs) ions into NFPP through HE strategy facilitates electron transportation by reducing the bandgap between the conduction and valence bands. Meanwhile, the 3D network Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion channels formed in HE-NFPP can effectively improve the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion efficiency, resulting in enhanced electrode reaction kinetics at low temperatures. Moreover, it leads to a significantly mitigated volume expansion/contraction in HE-NFPP caused by Na<sup>+</sup> ions extraction/insertion during (de)sodiation process. Consequently, the fabricated HE-NFPP exhibits an excellent rate and cyclic performance at low temperatures.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202502693-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202502693-sec-0010-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Recently, with increasingly serious environmental problems, the use of renewable energy has aroused much interest. However, these clean energy sources' inherent intermittency and randomness characteristics make them challenging for a continuous and stable power supply. Thus, large-scale energy storage techniques are required to promote their practical utilizations. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have acquired widespread applications for large-scale energy storage for the abundant sodium resources on the earth and their high safety and excellent operation temperature adaptability (−40–60&nbsp;°C) features.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0001_R_d16455662e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#anie202502693-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0002_R_d16455662e400" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Energy Chem.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cathode materials, the main factor determining the electrochemical performance of SIBs, have attracted significant research interests.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0003_R_d16455662e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Nano">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, selecting a cheap and durable cathode material is vital for the widespread commercialization of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Up to now, various SIB cathode materials have been developed, including layered metal oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0004_R_d16455662e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Stor. Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0005_R_d16455662e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Mater. Today">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Prussian blue materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0006_R_d16455662e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, the single crystalline O<sub>3</sub> type NaCrO<sub>2</sub> cathode materials reported by Peng et&nbsp;al. exhibit a capacity retention rate of 84.2% under 1.0 C at −10&nbsp;°C after 100 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0007_R_d16455662e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Mater. Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Prussian blue/carbon nanotube (CNT) composite materials reported by You et&nbsp;al. show a capacity retention of 85.0% under 0.1 C at −25&nbsp;°C compared to that at 25&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0008_R_d16455662e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (NFPP)/C cathode materials reported by Ge et&nbsp;al. demonstrate a discharge capacity of 74.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> under 0.5 C at −10&nbsp;°C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0009_R_d16455662e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Nano">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among these explored cathode materials, polyanionic compounds exhibit robust structural stability and low volume fluctuations for repeated Na<sup>+</sup> ions extraction/insertion cycles, making them highly promising for practical applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0010_R_d16455662e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a>-<a href="#anie202502693-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0012_R_d16455662e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Especially, the polyanionic NFPP cathode material has been regarded as one of the most promising SIB cathode materials due to its suitable working potential (≈3.1&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), high theoretical specific capacity of 128.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (1.0 C), and cost-effective characteristics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0013_R_d16455662e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the intrinsic electron conductivity and slow Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion kinetics of NFPP greatly hinder the interfacial charge exchange and ultimately adversely affect its electrochemical performance at low temperatures, particularly for the rate and cyclic performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0014_R_d16455662e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Stor. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Various methodologies have been adopted to tackle these problems, including elemental doping (e.g., Mg, Al, Cu, Mn),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0015_R_d16455662e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#anie202502693-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0018_R_d16455662e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew Chem. Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> carbonaceous materials coating,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0019_R_d16455662e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Stor. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and morphology controlling,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0020_R_d16455662e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the fabricated NFPP-based cathode materials exhibit enhanced electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>Currently, a high entropy (HE) strategy has been applied for secondary battery electrode materials fabrication. This strategy shows significant advantages in enhancing the electrode materials electrochemical performance by increasing electronic conductivity, reducing irreversible phase transitions, enhancing redox activity, and optimizing Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion pathways.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0021_R_d16455662e510" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The HE strategy is unique and involves introducing three or more elemental elements into the bulk crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0022_R_d16455662e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, the HE Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.85</sub>(Ni,Co,Mn,Cu,Mg)<sub>0.03</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (NFPP-HE) cathode materials reported by Zhang et&nbsp;al. exhibit improved ionic/electronic transportation capabilities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0023_R_d16455662e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, the HE Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>Co<sub>0.1</sub>Ni<sub>0.1</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) cathode materials reported by Jiang et&nbsp;al. exhibit enhanced charge transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0024_R_d16455662e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Small">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although these reported NFPP-based cathode materials exhibit excellent rate and cyclic performance, it is noted that their electrochemical performance at lower temperature ranges (−10 to −40&nbsp;°C) has rarely been reported.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0025_R_d16455662e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nano Energy">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, enhancing the electrochemical performance of NFPP by optimizing its intrinsic structural features remains a great challenge and an urgent desire.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, spherical high entropy (HE) cathode materials Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.95</sub>(MgCaAlCrMn)<sub>0.01</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (HE-NFPP) have been produced by utilizing a spray-drying combined with a solid-state reaction approach. The obtained HE-NFPP cathode materials demonstrate an excellent rate and cyclic performance at low temperatures. The transition metals (TMs) co-doping in NFPP through the HE strategy can effectively mitigate the volume changes and improve its electronic conductivity by reducing the bandgap between the conduction and valence bands. Meanwhile, the 3D network Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion channels formed in NFPP by TM ions co-doping through HE strategy facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion, leading to enhanced electrode reaction kinetics at low temperatures. The unique morphology and structure of fresh NFPP and HE-NFPP have been investigated to comprehend their influences on the advanced characteristics of HE-NFPP.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202502693-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202502693-sec-0020-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.95</sub>(MgCaAlCrMn)<sub>0.01</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (HE-NFPP) cathode materials were synthesized by employing a unique spray drying technique and high temperature annealing process, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0001">1</a>. Initially, the raw materials, carbon sources, and chelating agent oxalic acid are uniformly mixed in deionized (DI) water to obtain the solution for spray drying. The solubility of raw materials ensures that the materials can achieve accurate molecular-level mixing and chelating. Transition metals (TMs), e.g., Mg, Ca, Al, Cr, and Mn, have been incorporated into NFPP through the HE strategy. In the spray drying process, spherical hollow precursors have been obtained due to the droplet's uneven pressure distribution and low-viscosity of the raw materials. After the high temperature sintering process, spherical, honeycomb-shaped porous HE-NFPP particles were obtained. For comparison, pristine Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) (NFPP) cathode materials have been fabricated by similar processes.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202502693-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bc6cfbfe-9513-459c-8cca-07c48665f93b/anie202502693-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bc6cfbfe-9513-459c-8cca-07c48665f93b/anie202502693-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6fa27565-3c90-4e77-9fed-7aca61e26d3b/anie202502693-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bc6cfbfe-9513-459c-8cca-07c48665f93b/anie202502693-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202502693-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202502693" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The preparation flowchart for Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.95</sub>(MgCaAlCrMn)<sub>0.01</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (HE-NFPP) sample.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> shows the X-Ray Diffraction (XRD) patterns for NFPP and HE-NFPP, which belong to the oblique structure of Pn21a space group (JCPDS No. 97-023-6316).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0026_R_d16455662e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, these two samples exhibit identical diffraction peaks, indicating that HE TMs doping does not alter the structure of NFPP. It is apparent that the diffraction peak of (222) for HE-NFPP shifts toward the lower 2θ value, indicating the lattice expansion of NFPP. Rietveld refinement results presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2a,b</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> confirm the high crystallinity of NFPP and HE-NFPP. Crystal structures of NFPP and HE-NFPP materials are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2c</a>. As seen from the crystal structure of HE-NFPP, the [(Fe,M)<sub>3</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>13</sub>] layers parallel to the b-c axis are connected through the [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>] combination, forming a 3D network that can serve as highly efficient Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion channels. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> shows the Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) of NFPP and HE-NFPP. As shown in the figure, NFPP and HE-NFPP samples exhibit distinct characteristic peaks corresponding to PO<sub>4</sub> groups and P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>. Specifically, the characteristic peaks located at 400–700&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and 975–1200&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are related to O─P─O bending vibration and P-O tensile vibration in the PO<sub>4</sub> group, respectively. In addition, the peaks located at 719 and 963&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponded to symmetric and antisymmetric P─O─P vibrations in the P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> group.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0027_R_d16455662e732" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202502693-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4ba07924-c036-4655-b6ef-0aab30fbd8fd/anie202502693-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4ba07924-c036-4655-b6ef-0aab30fbd8fd/anie202502693-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1ac7a731-4f9b-470e-98f8-9127b7c62757/anie202502693-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4ba07924-c036-4655-b6ef-0aab30fbd8fd/anie202502693-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202502693-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202502693" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Rietveld refinement of the XRD pattern for NFPP a) and HE-NFPP b). Crystal structure of NFPP and HE-NFPP c). The SEM d), cross-sectional SEM (e), and HRTEM (f) image of NFPP. The SEM g), cross-sectional SEM h), and HRTEM i) images of HE-NFPP. SEM image and corresponding EDS elements mapping of HE-NFPP j). XANES spectra of Fe K-edge for NFPP and HE-NFPP k). The FT-EXAFS of NFPP and HE-NFPP l). The Wavelet Transform contour plots of NFPP m) and HE-NFPP n).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) photographs of NFPP and HE-NFPP displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2d,e,g,h</a>, and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> verify that the fabricated NFPP and HE-NFPP powders demonstrate a spherical particle morphology with a diameter of 5–10&nbsp;µm. The HE-NFPP sample exhibits an elevated compaction density of 2.30&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup> at 300&nbsp;MPa, a value significantly greater than the 1.98&nbsp;g&nbsp;cm<sup>−3</sup> density achieved for conventional NFPP at the same condition. The Focused Ion Beam (FIB) technique was utilized to study the internal structure of NFPP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2e</a>) and HE-NFPP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2h</a>). The interior of NFPP and HE-NFPP particles shows a porous honeycomb structure. High-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image indicates that a thin layer of carbon of ≈5&nbsp;nm is homogenously coated on the HE-NFPP particle surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2i</a>). While HRTEM in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2f</a> shows that the dimension of carbon thickness unevenly coated on the NFPP surface is ≈5–7&nbsp;nm. The carbon layer coated on NFPP and HE-NFPP surfaces has been further studied by using Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>). Two separately identifiable peaks at ≈1371&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (D-band) and 1573&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (G-band) have been observed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0010_R_d16455662e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a>-<a href="#anie202502693-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0012_R_d16455662e789" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value of the characteristic peak for HE-NFPP is 0.78, which is relatively lower than that of NFPP for 1.01, indicating that the partial graphitization carbon skeleton in HE-NFPP is expected to ensure a rapid electron transfer and eventually lead to an enhanced electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0028_R_d16455662e798" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2f</a> demonstrates that the lattice spacing of NFPP is 0.527&nbsp;nm, associated with the (110) crystal plane of NFPP. The lattice spacing shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2i</a> is 0.534&nbsp;nm, which can be matched with the (110) crystal plane of HE-NFPP. The lattice spacing for the (110) plane of HE-NFPP (0.534&nbsp;nm) is slightly larger than that of NFPP (0.522&nbsp;nm), which directly confirms that element doping in NFPP leads to an expansion in interlayer distance. The pertinent energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental distribution mapping results of the SEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2j</a>) and high-resolution TEM image (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>) indicate that Mg, Ca, Al, Cr, and Mn elements are uniformly distributed in the HE-NFPP sample.</p>
         
         <p>The effect of TM elements doping through HE strategy on the crystal structure of NFPP has been studied by using X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) analysis. The X-ray absorption near edge structure spectra (XANES) for NFPP and HE-NFPP are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0001">1k</a>, and the absorption Fe K-edge peak of HE-NFPP shifts slightly toward the lower energy, implying a marginal decrease in the unique oxidation state of Fe after TM ions doping through the HE strategy. The incorporation of HE TM ions causes changes in the coordination environment of Fe, which can be studied through the extended EXAFS of Fourier transform. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2l</a>, the Fe─O bond shifts slightly from 1.58 to 1.50&nbsp;Å, revealing that the strength of the Fe─O bond is improved and the structural stability of the crystal is further enhanced after the incorporation of TM ions through the HE strategy. Furthermore, the local environment of atoms can be more clearly identified through wavelet transform (WT). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2m,n</a>, the maximum wave number k of HE-NFPP is 4.16&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>, which is much lower than that of NFPP for 4.38&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>. It further indicates that HE-NFPP has a tighter, stronger Fe─O bond and enhanced structural stability compared to pristine NFPP.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0003">3a,b</a> present the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NFPP and HE-NFPP under a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> within 1.5–4.2&nbsp;V at 25&nbsp;°C. The initial oxidation peaks of the NFPP are centered at 2.662, 2.867, and 3.173&nbsp;V, related to the dissociation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The reduction peaks located at 2.840, 2.989, and 3.263&nbsp;V are related to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The process of Na<sup>+</sup> ions extraction in NFPP follows the order of Na3 (5-coordination)&nbsp;→&nbsp;Na1 (6-coordination)&nbsp;→&nbsp;Na4 (6-coordination), which corresponds to the transformation sequence Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>&nbsp;→&nbsp;Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>&nbsp;→&nbsp;NaFe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0029_R_d16455662e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Angew Chem. Int. Ed.">29</a>, <a href="#anie202502693-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0030_R_d16455662e885" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Stor. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the embedding of Na<sup>+</sup> ions has the opposite order. The oxidation-reduction peak potential difference (0.081&nbsp;V) corresponding to HE-NFPP is much smaller than the potential interpolation (0.090&nbsp;V) corresponding to NFPP. This phenomenon indicates that incorporating TM ions into NFPP leads to lower electrode polarization and enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202502693-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c14a82db-4782-4e9b-a36c-ebc66d0c111f/anie202502693-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c14a82db-4782-4e9b-a36c-ebc66d0c111f/anie202502693-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a9ec2b7e-09cf-44a0-9ae9-abd5fa3e22a4/anie202502693-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c14a82db-4782-4e9b-a36c-ebc66d0c111f/anie202502693-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202502693-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202502693" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CV curves of NFPP a) and HE-NFPP b) under a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> at 25&nbsp;°C. Charge and discharge curve of the first cycle at 0.1 C c), rate capability from 0.1 to 20 C d), EIS plots after 3 cycles at 0.1 C e), cyclic performance at 1.0 C f) and 20.0 C g) for NFPP and HE-NFPP at 25&nbsp;°C. Rate performance from 0.1 to 5.0 C (h) and cyclic performance i) under 1.0 C at −10&nbsp;°C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0003">3c</a> shows the typical galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves for NFPP and HE-NFPP conducted at 0.1 C (1 C&nbsp;=&nbsp;128.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) within a voltage spectrum from 1.5 to 4.2&nbsp;V. HE-NFPP exhibits a significantly elevated specific discharge capacity of 124.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, surpassing the particular capacity of the pristine NFPP for 115.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, compared to pristine NFPP, HE-NFPP shows a significantly improved rate performance, as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0003">3d</a>. HE-NFPP exhibits high reversible discharge capacities of 124.8, 113.7, 106.8, 103.9, 100.0, 95.8, and 90.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0, and 20.0 C, respectively. Upon reverting the current parameter to 0.1C, its discharge capacity can quickly recover to 121.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. While NFPP only delivers the discharge capacities of 114.8, 105.5, 100.8, 95.8, 90.6, 86.1, and 80.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0, and 20.0 C, respectively. However, its discharge capacity can only return back to 112.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> upon reversion of the current density to 1.0 C. To further study the influence of HE TMs doping on Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion kinetics in HE-NFPP, EIS spectra have been collected (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0003">3e</a>) and analyzed by an equivalent circuit model (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>). The fitting results are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>. HE-NFPP exhibits a much lower R<sub>ct</sub> for 150.1&nbsp;Ω than that of NFPP for 310.6&nbsp;Ω, highlighting the benefit of incorporation of TM ions into polyanionic NFPP using the HE strategy, which can facilitate the electrons transportation by reducing the bandgap between the conduction and valence bands and eventually enhance its intrinsic electronic conductivity. Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion coefficient in HE-NFPP is much higher than that in NFPP (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>), which is consistent with the GITT calculation results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0003">3f</a> shows the cyclic performance of HE-NFPP and NFPP under 1.0 C at 25&nbsp;°C. The discharge capacity of HE-NFPP is much higher than pristine NFPP, but no noticeable discharge capacity degradation can be observed for both samples under 1.0 C after 100 cycles. Moreover, HE-NFPP can still deliver an impressive discharge capacity of 82.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 5000 cycles at 20.0 C, maintaining an outstanding capacity retention of 93.0% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0003">3g</a>). In contrast, NFPP only exhibits a discharge capacity of 60.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 20.0 C after 5000 cycles and a capacity retention of 75.8%.</p>
         
         <p>Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion activity in the electrolyte decreases at low temperatures, resulting in sluggish electrode reaction kinetics. Therefore, studying the effects of TM ions doping in NFPP through HE strategy on its electrochemical performance at low temperature operation conditions is of great significance. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0003">3h</a> shows the rate performance of NFPP and HE-NFPP at −10&nbsp;°C. The depicted data reveal that HE-NFPP demonstrates superior reversible discharge capacities of 108.4, 99.0, 91.0, 86.1, 74.5, and 63.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 3.0, and 5.0 C, respectively. When the current returns to 0.1 C, HE-NFPP still maintains a capacity of 104.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Correspondingly, the discharge capacities of NFPP are only 84.6, 66.8, 55.7, 41.2, 30.8, and 19.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 3.0, and 5.0 C, respectively. When the current is set back to 0.1 C, the discharge capacity of NFPP is only 60.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. More importantly, HE-NFPP exhibits a remarkable discharge capacity of 84.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles under 1.0 C at −10&nbsp;°C, with a noticeable capacity retention of 96.7% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0003">3i</a>). In comparison, NFPP only exhibits a discharge capacity of 38.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 1.0 C at −10&nbsp;°C, and the capacity retention of NFPP is only 65.6%. The enhanced electrochemical performance of HE-NFPP at low temperatures is primarily due to the 3D network Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion channels formed by TM ions doping through HE strategy in HE-NFPP, which can effectively enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion kinetics at low temperatures.</p>
         
         <p>To understand how the HE TM ions incorporation affects the structural and cyclic stability of NFPP, real-time in situ XRD was conducted to further explore the structural evolution during the initial charging–discharging cycle, as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0004">4a–d</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0004">4a,b</a> indicates that the characteristic diffraction peaks for pristine NFPP and HE-NFPP, for example, (011), (210), (013), (022), (222) and (602), remain distinct, demonstrating that both NFPP and HE-NFPP exhibits good structure reversibility during the charge–discharge process. As the charging proceeds, the (011), (210), (022), and (222) peaks for NFPP shift to the lower 2θ value, indicating an expansion of the interplanar spacing during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0031_R_d16455662e1012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As it is charged to 3.2&nbsp;V, the (013) peak shifts suddenly to the higher 2θ. This phenomenon is related to the process of Na<sup>+</sup> ions deintercalation and can be explained by the deformation of the [P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>] unit in NFPP when x is less than 2 in Na<sub>x</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, indicating the contraction of the unit cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0032_R_d16455662e1037" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Chem. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The (222) peak splits gradually into two peaks and recovers to its original position at the end of the discharging process. Moreover, the diffraction peak of (602) quickly disappears as it is charged to 3.1&nbsp;V and reappears when the discharge is through. A similar phenomenon has been observed in HE-NFPP; it demonstrates high crystal structure stability of NFPP and HE-NFPP, and the whole (de)sodiation process is highly reversible in NFPP and HE-NFPP. As indicated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0004">4a</a>, it should be pointed out that the 2θ value changes for (011), (013), and (022) peaks in NFPP are 0.292, 0.524, and 0.475°, respectively. But the 2θ value changes for (011), (013), and (022) peaks of HE-NFPP are decreased to 0.245, 0.339, and 0.475°, respectively. Accordingly, the lattice parameters (<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, <i>c</i>) and volume (<i>V</i>) evolutions have been studied based on in situ XRD measurements, and the results are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0004">4c,d</a>. As seen from the figures, NFPP suffers from a volume change of 5.77% during the charging process, while the volume change for HE-NFPP is only 3.98%. It is attributed that the 3D network Na&nbsp;+&nbsp;ions diffusion channels formed in HE-NFPP can effectively improve the Na&nbsp;+&nbsp;ions diffusion efficiency (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0002">2c</a>), resulting in the enhanced electrode reaction kinetics and lower volume change. It demonstrates that incorporating TM ions through the HE strategy can efficiently enhance the structural stability of NFPP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0033_R_d16455662e1064" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The in-depth X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectra have been conducted to clarify the Al, Ca, Cr, Mg, and Mn distribution in HE-NFPP (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> indicates that they are evenly distributed in HE-NFPP. Meanwhile, it reveals that the incorporation of Mg, Ca, Al, Cr, and Mn elements in NFPP does not affect the peak position and intensity of Fe 2p and P 2p.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202502693-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5d08ae3c-4a45-4268-925a-7b359d838650/anie202502693-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5d08ae3c-4a45-4268-925a-7b359d838650/anie202502693-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f6205aeb-f683-4723-92c1-6c9daf7b165e/anie202502693-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5d08ae3c-4a45-4268-925a-7b359d838650/anie202502693-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202502693-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202502693" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The charge–discharge profiles and in situ XRD patterns of NFPP a) and HE-NFPP b), and the corresponding volume (V) evolutions of NFPP (c) and HE-NFPP d).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To fully assess the feasibility of HE-NFPP in practical applications, the SIB pouch-type full cells were assembled by coupling the HE-NFPP cathode with hard carbon (HC) anode, denoted as HE-NFPP||HC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0005">5a</a>). The CV curves and GCD profiles of HE-NFPP cathode and HC anode used to fabricate the pouch-type full cells are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0005">5b,c</a>. The N/P ratio of the assembled HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cells is set to 1.2. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> shows the digital photo of the assembled HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell. For comparison, the NFPP||HC pouch-type full cells were constructed with an NFPP cathode and an HC anode. Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> shows the main design parameters of these two types of full cells. These pouch-type full cells had been activated before testing. The first charge–discharge curves for NFPP||HC and HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cells under 0.08&nbsp;A at 25&nbsp;°C are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>. The discharge capacity of HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell is 391&nbsp;mAh under 0.1 C within 1.5–3.7&nbsp;V at 25&nbsp;°C, while the discharge capacity of NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell is 373&nbsp;mAh. Rate performance of the NFPP||HC and HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cells at 25&nbsp;°C are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>. The HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell exhibits an excellent rate performance and delivers the reversible capacities of 381.5, 370.3, 351.8, 317.5, 290.2, and 246.0&nbsp;mAh under 0.08, 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, 1.2, and 2.0&nbsp;V, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>). Even at 2.0&nbsp;A, the discharge capacity of the HE-NFPP||HC remained 246.0&nbsp;mAh, which is superior to that of the NFPP||HC for 180.8&nbsp;mAh. As anticipated, the HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full-cell demonstrates a considerably high Na<sup>+</sup>-storage performance at low temperatures. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0005">5d</a>, HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell exhibits high discharge capacities of 408, 394, 391, 365, 346, and 305&nbsp;mAh under 0.08 A at 45, 35, 25, 0, −20, and −40&nbsp;°C, respectively. It still exhibits a high capacity retention rate of 80.3% at −40&nbsp;°C (compared to 25&nbsp;°C). However, the discharge capacity of the NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell can only reach 387, 380, 373, 314, 310, and 255&nbsp;mAh under 0.08&nbsp;A at 45, 35, 25, 0, −20, and −40&nbsp;°C, respectively. The NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell can only exhibit a capacity retention rate of 70.8% at −40&nbsp;°C (compared to 25&nbsp;°C). Moreover, the HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell exhibits an excellent cyclic performance at −10&nbsp;°C. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0005">5e</a> indicates a capacity retention rate of 97.4% after 100 cycles under 0.08&nbsp;A at −10&nbsp;°C. However, the NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell only shows a capacity retention rate of 90.3% after 100 cycles under 0.08&nbsp;A at −10&nbsp;°C. The long-term cyclic performance of HE-NFPP||HC and NFPP||HC pouch-type full cells have also been studied at 25&nbsp;°C. The results are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0005">5f</a>, The HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell indicates a high capacity retention rate of 95.1% after 500 cycles at 0.4&nbsp;A, which is much higher than that of the NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell for 57.7%. The excellent electrochemical performance at low temperatures of the HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell demonstrates that the designed HE-NFPP is a prospective cathode material for high-performance SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202502693-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/57b07de3-5188-4fa4-9f4b-6e92a55a7b31/anie202502693-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/57b07de3-5188-4fa4-9f4b-6e92a55a7b31/anie202502693-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/442aee14-2f92-4056-85e8-80da3b592c55/anie202502693-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/57b07de3-5188-4fa4-9f4b-6e92a55a7b31/anie202502693-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202502693-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202502693" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the HE-NFPP||HC pouch-type full cell a). The CV curves b) and GCD profiles c) of HE-NFPP cathode and hard carbon (HC) anode. Discharge capacities under 0.08&nbsp;A at different temperatures d) and cyclic performance under 0.08&nbsp;A at −10&nbsp;°C e) and under 0.4&nbsp;A at 25&nbsp;°C f) for the HE-NFPP| |HC and NFPP||HC pouch-type full cells.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To gain insights into the enhanced structural stability of NFPP and HE-NFPP during cycling, the XPS spectra of C 1s, F 1s, and O 1s for NFPP and HE-NFPP electrodes after 5000 cycles under 20.0 C have been studied, and the relevant data is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0006">6a–c</a>. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0006">6a</a>, the C 1s spectra for NFPP and HE-NFPP can be deconvoluted into four peaks, C─C, C─O, C═O, and O─C═O. The stronger C─C bond is mainly attributed to the additives and binders in the electrode, while the small peaks of C─O, C═O, and O─C═O bonds are corresponded to the by-products of electrolyte decomposition. In the F 1s spectra of NFPP and HE-NFPP, the two peaks centered at 687.2 and 684.8&nbsp;eV are associated with the PVDF and NaF, respectively, where NaF is attributed to the product of the side reaction between PVDF and NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0006">6b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0018_R_d16455662e1167" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew Chem. Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The characteristic peaks of ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> centered at 534.1 and 532.5&nbsp;eV are observed in the O<span style="font-family:monospace">&nbsp;</span>1&nbsp;s spectra of NFPP and HE-NFPP, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0006">6c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0034_R_d16455662e1186" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Angew Chem. Int. Ed.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C─O and C═O peaks in C1s, the NaF peak in F1s, and the ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na peak in O1s of NFPP are much stronger than those in HE-NFPP, indicating that more organic components produced by electrolyte decomposition appeared on the surface of NFPP. The XPS analysis results suggest that TM elements doped in NFPP through HE strategy demonstrate a capability to robustly stabilize the cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) and inhibit electrolyte decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#anie202502693-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202502693-bib-0035_R_d16455662e1193" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Stor. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202502693-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/178f1a87-f503-45e3-a8f5-136e6ca2367e/anie202502693-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/178f1a87-f503-45e3-a8f5-136e6ca2367e/anie202502693-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/be67ee4f-35ec-41d6-b39d-386c847699e7/anie202502693-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/178f1a87-f503-45e3-a8f5-136e6ca2367e/anie202502693-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202502693-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202502693" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS spectra of C 1s a), F 1s b), and O 1s c) for NFPP and HE-NFPP after 500 cycles at 0.4&nbsp;A in the pouch-type full cells. SEM images of NFPP before d) and after e), and SEM images of HE-NFPP before f) and after g), 500 cycles at 0.4&nbsp;A in the pouch-type full cells. Schematically illustration of structural stability of NFPP and HE-NFPP particle after long-term cyclic testing h).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0006">6d–g</a> shows the morphology of NFPP and HE-NFPP cathodes in the pouch-type full cells before and after cycling. After 500 cycles at 0.4&nbsp;A, the spherical particles of HE-NFPP are well preserved despite the appearance of small cracks on the surface. In contrast, NFPP particles showed significant fragmentation, reflecting the structural collapse caused by uneven stress/strain within the particles during cycling and indicating that TM doping through the HE strategy can effectively strengthen the structural stability of NFPP cathode materials. The above analysis shows that the structural changes of particles during the cycling process are different between NFPP and HE-NFPP, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#anie202502693-fig-0006">6h</a>. The in situ XRD results suggest that the lattice stress in HE-NFPP is effectively alleviated through HE TM co-doping, and the volume change is reduced to 3.98%. In addition, the unique honeycomb-like porous structure has a stable structure and adequate void space, which can effectively alleviate the volume expansion of cathode particles during cycling due to the continuous insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, ultimately improving the structural stability of HE-NFPP.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202502693-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202502693-sec-0030-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the spherical high entropy (HE) Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>2.95</sub>(MgCaAlCrMn)<sub>0.01</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (HE-NFPP) cathode materials have been successfully synthesized via a spray-drying and high-temperature sintering process. Benefitting from the incorporation of transition metals (TMs) in NFPP through the HE strategy, the bandgap between conduction and valence bands is significantly reduced, ensuring rapid electron transportation and enhancing electronic conductivity. Meanwhile, the 3D network Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion channels formed in NFPP by TMs doping through HE strategy can effectively improve the Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion efficiency, resulting in enhanced electrode reaction kinetics at low temperatures. Moreover, it leads to a mitigated volume expansion/contraction of HE-NFPP caused by Na<sup>+</sup> ions extraction/insertion during (de)sodiation process. Eventually, the obtained HE-NFPP cathode materials demonstrate an excellent rate and cyclic performance at low temperatures. This study provides a feasible direction for developing advanced cathode materials for SIBs and promoting their industrial applications.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
